According to a telegram from Jining on the fourteenth, on the night of the thirteenth, bandit cavalry fiercely attacked the defensive positions in eastern Suiyuan. It was not until four o'clock yesterday morning that they began to retreat. Nine planes from a certain side reconnoitered Pingdiquan. According to a telegram from Guihua on the fifteenth, military officers from a certain side participated in the bandit forces. A skirmish occurred in eastern Suiyuan, and the bandits are busily transporting supplies and actively preparing for military action. This kind of behavior cannot be regarded as just ordinary bandits.

However, there are two aspects of the aid-to-Suiyuan movement that deserve the most attention: one is material assistance, and the other is practical assistance. Our people are capable of material assistance, but our people are not capable of practical assistance. According to previous news, the defensive work in Suiyuan is extremely solid, and the equipment is also very sufficient. However, the problem of flour is still a shortage, and military uniforms are also needed. How can we bear to see these soldiers who are defending the country and protecting the people go hungry on the front lines and freeze to death in the ditches without quickly taking action, opening our purses, and providing assistance? Therefore, various places in Tianjin and Beijing took the lead in launching the aid-to-Suiyuan movement, and various places in Nanjing and Shanghai followed suit. I think everyone has a patriotic heart, and this kind of aid-to-Suiyuan movement will soon spread throughout the country, which is undoubtedly a matter of little concern.

However, according to the statement from friendly countries, the current bandit army's attack is unrelated to them and is entirely a matter of internal affairs. Is this statement credible? According to a telegram from Jining, there were nine planes from a certain side reconnoitering Pingdiquan; according to a telegram from Guihua, military officers from a certain side participated in the bandit forces in combat. Therefore, it is clear that the bandit disaster in Suiyuan is not just an invasion by bandits. Furthermore, looking at their plan, since the entire negotiation failed, they are using the method of invading bit by bit. In this way, they can use less force and achieve more success. If we only rely on the Suiyuan army and do not provide assistance from other armies, it is extremely dangerous. Therefore, the current aid to Suiyuan not only requires material assistance, but also practical assistance from various places, which is especially important and urgent. All of our people should rise up to do the Save-Suiyuan movement, and all of our soldiers should rise up to do the Rescue-Suiyuan movement.

On Reading Newspapers

— Ziying

In this turbulent era, countless new events occur around the world every day. Due to the development of modern journalism and the global spread of news networks, news agencies around the world select the most important events that have a direct or indirect impact on all of humanity and provide them to newspapers for publication. Furthermore, due to the close organization of all political and economic institutions in the international community, their importance is further increased. For example, after Roosevelt's re-election as President of the United States, political commentators from various countries rushed to evaluate its impact on their own countries. France's abandonment of the gold standard caused scholars from various countries to study its impact on their own economies. Therefore, if you are a member of human society in the 20th century and want to understand the era in which you live, the country to which you belong, and the importance of your position in your own country, you must read the newspaper every day. Otherwise, your existence is blind, and you will become a laggard in the times. Therefore, reading the newspaper is a job that everyone who can read the newspaper should do every day. It can be said to be a part of daily life that is as important as eating every day. In particular, the Chinese people today, when the country is at a critical juncture of life and death, every citizen should pay close attention to the evolution of serious situations, such as the Sino-Japanese negotiations currently taking place in Nanjing and the conflicts between the Suiyuan garrison and the Meng puppet troops. Only after thoroughly understanding these events can they express legitimate public opinion. This kind of public opinion is a great force to urge the government, and the government cannot and dare not ignore it.

However, the actual situation in China is really too pitiful. With a population of 470 million, the circulation of the largest daily newspaper is only over 100,000 copies per day. Compared with the circulation of millions of copies of well-known newspapers in the United States, we are really pitifully small. Even compared to Japan, the circulation of some important newspapers is over one million copies. On the one hand, this is due to the lack of widespread education in our country and the large number of illiterate people. On the other hand, many people who have received education, even university students who have received higher education, do not have the habit of reading newspapers, which is really distressing. The situation is even worse for women. Few women read newspapers while they are in school, and once they leave school and get married, they are simply cut off from newspapers. This is a common fact before our eyes that no one can deny. But we should know that our female compatriots make up half of our country's population! Although the number of female compatriots who know how to read newspapers is already very small, the fact that this small number of people who can read newspapers do not read newspapers is indeed a serious problem that deserves our attention. As for the universalization of education, that is another issue, which will be discussed later. People in every family are often very heavily influenced by the mistress of the house. If the mistress of the house can enthusiastically read the newspaper and pay attention to domestic and international political and economic trends, then everyone else will also be influenced by her. In short, we should launch a newspaper-reading movement so that everyone who can read the newspaper reads the newspaper every day. Those who do not have the ability to read the newspaper should also find ways to make them aware of the facts published in the newspaper. I believe that this is just like the "universal mobilization of the intelligence of all the people," which is called by the Japanese military in their recently published booklet as the most important thing for Japan today. It will definitely have a great effect on spiritual national defense.

Street Cries on a Cold Night

— Liao Chuxiu

Mr. Zhou Zuoren, in his essay "Street Cries at the End of the Year," mentioned the deep, sorrowful calls of those selling "hard-crust steamed buns" in Beijing on winter nights, which caused those of us who have never been to Beijing to abandon our books and let our imaginations run wild. This, like the passage by Nagai Kafu on ukiyo-e prints, "the paper lantern of the soba noodle vendor shines lonely on the riverside at night," pleases me because at night I often forget the existence of the world and myself. But "street cries late at night," whether the sounds of small businesses or relying on one's ears or eyes, make me feel that I am still a part of this organic world.

Street cries late at night are probably mostly from those selling food for late-night snacks. Those who have finished their work during the day chat idly with friends late into the night and suddenly realize that it is very late. At that moment, they feel a little hungry, and just then someone selling late-night snacks passes by. His calls startle those who are talking, and they go out to buy something to eat, which is really very interesting.

We do not know when the selling of hard-crust steamed buns began in Beijing. In the south, there are now many things to eat for late-night snacks, but it seems that only malt sugar has been recorded.

Gu Lu's "Qing Jia Lu" mentions malt sugar in the ninth month:

"Scholars boil rice with malt sprouts to make sugar, which is called malt sugar. It is sold on cold nights, and the sound of gongs echoes through the streets, extremely desolate. Cai Yun's Wu Ballads says: 'Dark mist has enveloped us for three days, preparing for the west wind to blow proudly tonight; the cold air arrives as tin is sold in the deep alleys, the sound of the chime is nothing like a flute.'"

Cai Yun's poem cannot completely shake off its scholarly air. But Gu Lu's "the sound of gongs echoes, extremely desolate through the streets," allows us to get a sense of the bleakness of the west wind and the sound of gongs rising in the cold night.

"餳 (Táng)," is called "飴 (Yí)" in the annotations to the Chu Ci. That is the ancient "銀鍠 (Yín Huáng)." The people of Wu call it sugar. The reason it is only sold between autumn and winter is because at that time, "the wind is dry and the sugar is brittle, which is good for people's teeth." The Suzhou Prefecture Chronicle says, "The malt sugar that comes from Zhitang Market in Changshu is called scallion tube sugar; the malt sugar that comes from Kunshan and is shaped like a triangular prism is called hemp zong sugar." And the Wu Ballads annotation even says: Selling "餳 (Táng)" on winter nights is for those who work at night "to relieve their hunger." Qian Xingxi's poem from Jiashan says, "The shoulder pole carrying 餳 (Táng) is sold at night, we hear the gong." The reason for buying 餳 (Táng) on cold nights in Wu is not for the idle to have a late-night snack, but for the "night workers" to relieve their hunger. This makes us feel a sense of the "human touch of labor."

Now, those who buy 餳 (Táng) are no longer limited to the cold nights of Wu. In the alleys of Shanghai, you can see people buying 餳 (Táng) who are knocking on "hand gongs" at any time. However, most of them no longer buy with money, but exchange it for waste goods such as rags and scrap iron.

Since the sound of "hard-crust steamed buns" in Beijing is so bleak and sorrowful that it pierces people's hearts, and the sound of buying 餳 (Táng) in Wu on cold nights is also "extremely desolate in the streets," this is nothing more than the result of the hardships of life and the loneliness of the floating world.

In Shanghai, there are "street cries at night" very often. But the night in the city is already no different from the day, so we cannot easily find street cries that are different from the day. The sound of Guangdong people selling olives will make even the most patient people angry. The sound of selling ginkgo nuts in early autumn is only slow and melodious, and is no longer a "soul's cry." As for the singing of those selling foreign goods, it makes those who hear it immediately unhappy, and they have already reached the point of using bad singing to force people to buy.

As I approach middle age, the heroic spirit of youth is completely gone, and I pay special attention to these small things. "How great is my decline!"

Autumn is deep, and what follows is the long winter night. When will I be able to go to Wu and hear the sound of the gong selling 餳 (Táng)? When will I be able to go to Beijing and hear the cries of "hard-crust steamed buns"? I have really had enough of the sound of buying foreign goods!

The People Underground

— Sansan

—Coal Miners in Guizhou—

One mountain connects to another, without interruption, without end, putting the entire province of Guizhou in the embrace of mountains. In these countless mountains, inexhaustible resources are buried.

Coal is one of the most important.

Its deposits are mostly found at the base of sturdy mountains with sparse vegetation. Those who operate coal mines dig a hole at the foot of the mountain, sloping downwards, directly reaching the coal deposit. Sometimes this can extend for several miles.

You can only crawl into the cave bent over, but after only a few steps, you must crawl like a dog. These sloping underground tunnels are generally only half a person high and two people wide. Inside, you cannot see clearly, not even your own body. You must use your hands as "antennae" to listen to whether you should turn, otherwise, your head will rudely bump into the rocks. There is often some water accumulated inside, making it very slippery. Small pits have only one underground path, while large pits open up many branches depending on the amount of coal reserves and capital.

There are several types of workers in the coal mine. One type is those who dig coal at the bottom of the pit. This area is relatively higher and wider than the tunnels, but the coal miners must often lie on their backs to drill into the coal seams.

In the coal mine, as most people imagine, it is filled with pungent coal gas. In reality, however, there is no coal gas at all, because this is "raw coal." Therefore, what makes the workers suffer is a lamp. This lamp uses tung oil, and the wick is almost as thick as a little finger, constantly releasing tung oil smoke. Since the air in the cave is not circulating, new workers feel even more distressed by this smell. As soon as they crawl in, their lungs feel like they are being propped up by something, making it difficult to breathe. And within minutes, their heads feel like they are being stuffed with something tight. But they have to work eight hours in the pit. Moreover, those who work at the bottom of the pit often face the possibility of accidents. If the coal block above their heads loosens and collapses, they will be buried. If they are lucky, they will only lose a hand or a foot. The bosses say this is "bad luck," and at most, they will give a piece of "fired wood board" as compensation.

Every day, they spend eight hours without seeing the sun, breathing tung oil smoke, and trying their best to dig into the hard coal mine, but they only earn a little over one string of cash (approximately equivalent to over three dimes).

Another type of worker is the coal pullers. Their job is to pull the dug coal out of the cave.

The tool for pulling coal is a bamboo basket, which they call a "boat." This boat can hold one or two hundred pounds of coal. Each time, they have to drag this one or two hundred pounds of coal out like a dog pulling a cart, and the road is pitch black and uneven. So when they crawl, they often whistle. This is to notify the people coming ahead to stop in a relatively wide place so that they can drag the coal past. Their wages are less than those of the coal diggers, only about one string of cash a day.

In the cave, regardless of spring, summer, autumn, or winter, they are all naked, without wearing any clothes. Even the coal pullers rarely wear pants.

These two types of workers are the most numerous. Usually, even a single tunnel has dozens of workers (working in three shifts). In places with many branches, the number increases to hundreds. The number of workers working on the surface is relatively small.

One type is the fan operators, who send air from a small hole that has been dug out to the bottom of the pit. This is necessary so that the lamps can be lit and people can live.

There are also the coal-burning workers. The place where coal is burned is next to the entrance of the cave, in a circular shape with a diameter of about thirty feet. Every morning, around the coal-burning area, men, women, and even eleven- or twelve-year-old children stand, waiting for the coal to be burned (cooked coal) and transported to the city dozens of miles away to be sold. They endure the coal smoke swirling around the valley, letting that peculiar odor sting their noses.

Nearby, one straw shed after another is built, serving as the workers' dwellings. Inside the houses, the floor is completely covered with rice straw. A room of only three square feet often sleeps more than a dozen people. In their dwellings, you can always find several opium lamps. Almost every coal mine worker is addicted to opium. Without smoking, they cannot eliminate that excessive fatigue.

There are tens of thousands of these workers in Guizhou, but because they are working with primitive methods, although they exert excessive effort, the amount of coal produced is still limited, so that many local residents still do not have coal to burn.

The First Day in Saigon (Continued)

— Liu Ming

However, another half hour passed, and the people were still baking in the scorching sun? Large beads of sweat streamed down their faces, and the shirts of those wearing short sleeves were completely soaked with sweat. Suddenly, a uniformed Chinese man appeared and shouted, "Hey, line up, line up!"

People looked at him bewildered, but still instinctively crowded together to form two lines. Many men with families led their wives and children, but those wearing Western clothes shouted:

"Women go to the women's line, men go to the men's line!" The voice was extraordinarily fierce.

"Sir, I don't know the way! What's wrong with us being together? We already have three children!"

Ten minutes later, we were lined up in two lines, with the women's line going first. We followed behind. Many people with guns and leather whips and rattan canes stood on both sides of us. But as soon as this makeshift line stepped off the drawbridge, it immediately fell into chaos. Many women wanted to turn back to look for their husbands, and many men wanted to go forward to find their wives. Some people wanted to run ahead, and some people wanted to go out to say a few words to their acquaintances... So the fierce whips fell like raindrops on everyone's heads, along with crude language and shouts. When the crowd saw the whips being raised, they covered their heads, avoided the blows, and squeezed together. On the road, the shouts, cries, and the dull sound of whips landing on flesh, like red-hot iron bars falling into water, merged into one.

My hopes were dashed. My back was whipped twice with a heavy whip. In the crowd, I unconsciously burst into tears. I thought: This is just like a group of slaves of a conquered country being whipped as they are led to the execution ground, and towards the distant, once despised "Tangshan" (China), a feeling of love naturally arose.

Fifteen minutes later, we stopped in front of a Western-style building. Thinking about all the future hardships, I couldn't help but tremble.

Buddha's Voice (980) 9:00 PM to 9:45 PM

Mei Luo Xiang

Mei Luo Xiang has been back in Shanghai for six months and hasn't had any roles. Initially, she had some savings and jewelry to pawn to get by, but as time passed, she was running out of resources and facing financial problems. She hadn't paid rent for three months.

Qin Si Tian, a male role singer who lived in the back room, was merely a friend and colleague. He had also recently lost his job, so he often came to Mei Luo Xiang's room to chat. Once, he mentioned the major shareholder of the Ming Xin Stage, Bai Sen Qing, and encouraged her to ask him for help.

Mei Luo Xiang was almost moved by his words, but seeing Ma Zi Ying's letters arriving daily, saying that the future wasn't entirely hopeless, she felt as if she'd received a shot of adrenaline and didn't want to surrender to Bai Sen Qing.

Bai Sen Qing also knew about her recent financial difficulties and bribed the morally corrupt actress Xiao Chun Lan to tempt Mei Luo Xiang with various methods. Mei Luo Xiang, like many women in society, sometimes seemed very rational, but other times couldn't resist the lure of material things. In the end, she surrendered to Bai Sen Qing's trap.

Mei Luo Xiang and Bai Sen Qing lived together for two months, and they both seemed to be growing tired of each other.

Suddenly, Mei Luo Xiang received an urgent letter from Ma Zi Ying. He had struck it rich by opening a gold mine on Gulangyu Island and was coming to Shanghai today to fulfill their vows to each other. This put Mei Luo Xiang in a dilemma!

She knelt before Bai Sen Qing and begged him to allow her to go with Ma Zi Ying. Bai Sen Qing initially pretended to refuse, but later agreed. In reality, his enthusiasm for Mei Luo Xiang had long cooled.

But would Ma Zi Ying forgive her momentary lapse of weakness?…

Opening: "Lament of a Poor Woman"

Snowflakes dance and fill the sky. Xu Niang's heart is in a mess. Alone by the cold window, she often sighs sadly. She remembers when she was a delicate girl in the Tian family. Her parents loved her like a treasure. There was nothing she couldn't have, save the moon in the sky. Her meals were carefully chosen, and in winter she wore brocade and in summer she wore silk. Her parents arranged her marriage, hoping for a harmonious and loving couple. But unexpectedly, her husband was a pleasure-seeking wastrel. He didn't attend to his business, only wanting to chase after flowers. Furthermore, his parents were addicted to opium. The family business declined, making it difficult to make ends meet. Now, as the cold wind blows and the day grows colder, she lacks clothing and food, causing her suffering. Her children cry pitifully before her, wearing thin clothes with little cotton. Her heart is heavy, and tears fall. She hates her husband's crooked heart. He wastes money seeking pleasure, but does he know that there is no rice or firewood at home? He chats happily with courtesans in warm rooms, while she sits here by the dim lamp, practicing embroidery. She pities herself for working tirelessly and sacrificing her delicate body. She laments that she earns so little money that it's hard to support the family. Furthermore, the children are too young to study. She pities herself for being a poor and miserable woman. Moreover, she has borrowed from relatives and neighbors, and has no choice but to return to her mother's home. She doesn't care that the weather is cold and the road is slippery.

Interesting Tidbits from "Female Students"

Leng Bo learned to push a trolley in "By the Huangpu River", and plays a carpenter in "Female Students". Every day he also learns crafts such as pushing a borer and sawing wood. Director Wen said to him: "If you make a few more movies and learn all kinds of crafts, even if you don't eat the movie meal, you won't have to worry about having the ability to eat!"

Jiang Jingcheng's hair is already bald, and it shines like a mirror. The cameraman is most afraid of the light reflected from the top of his head into the lens when shooting his scenes.

Wang Huijuan had a special surgery performed by a beauty doctor because her nose bridge was too low. The bridge of her nose has risen several points and is much more beautiful than before.

Fan Xuepeng plays a blind woman. One day, she was practicing playing a blind eye, and Chen Yumei came to give her a roll of banknotes, and she immediately took it and put it in her pocket. Everyone laughed at her for being greedy for money.

Tao Jin and Wang Huijuan don't know how to guess punches, but there is a scene where the two have to guess punches. Director Wen taught them temporarily, but when they went on camera, Wang Huijuan often stretched out two fingers and called "Eight Immortals", and stretched out four fingers and called "Two Good". Many mistakes were made, and many N.G. were taken.

"Star" Volume 7, Issue 3 Published

The third issue of the seventh volume of the Star Biweekly has been published. This issue is a special issue of "Qingming Festival", and the form and content are more progressive and enriched. The cover is a photo of Li Minghui and Zhao Dan, an introduction to the highlights of "Qingming Festival" in ten pictures, four photos of "United in Life and Death", photos of Hu Die and Nanjing shooting exterior scenes from "Eternal Smile", new photos of Xie Jun, Gu Lanjun, Gong Jianong, Shu Xiuwen, etc., Xu Xinfu's new work "Lively Dragon and Tiger" and Shen Xiling's new work "Crossroads", etc. In addition to the semi-monthly comics, there are also works by famous artists such as Chen Xiaoyin, Chen Haoxiong, Jiang Suiliang, and Zizimei. The articles include Ouyang Yuqian's "Actors in Qingming Festival", Yao Xinong's "About Qingming Festival", Hu Xinling's "The Story of the Jar". "Biweekly Gossip" includes Xu Jia's "Starting with Dancing to Save the Country" and Beihai's "About Newsreels". Short news clips and descriptions of exterior scenes in "The Smiling New Capital", introductions to new films in "Miscellaneous Writings on United in Life and Death", etc. The retail price is still six cents per copy.
